                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="5c953c9712724ac4bbb49b0af949e46f">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c0bbe375-3e60-4008-81b5-b0cf1879d3bf'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d203940526" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sulfur doping in carbonaceous anode is a popular method to improve the sodium storing performance. Regulating the C/S bond effect of carbon atom with doped sulfur atom could understand and develop superior carbonaceous anode for sodium-ion batteries. Therefore, this study designed porous hollow carbon nanocages with synergistically-doped C/S bonds (C-S/C = S) by secondary-sulfidation. The secondary-sulfidation is conducive to the different bond formation of sulfur atom with carbon skeleton and adjust the structure via removed other groups. Thus, the C-S/C = S anode with synergistically C-S and C = S bonds delivered a reversible capacity of 307 mAh g-1 at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> with initial coulomb efficiency of 80.15%, a stable cycle life of 2000 cycles, and a fast charge capability of 186 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> in sodium-ion batteries. Through characterization and simulation results, the doped C-S/C = S bonds creates more active sites and ion diffusion channels in carbon skeleton for enhancing the fast and durable kinetics of sodium-ion. This work provides deep insights into C/S bonds effect of carbon anode material for developing fast charge stability sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202400020-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400020-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium is an alkali metal with similar properties to lithium, and sodium is much more abundant on Earth than lithium, thus, sodium as a substitute for lithium has emerged and has received more and more attention in the battery field for reducing the pressure on lithium resource limitations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0001_R_d203940518e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater. Small Stru. Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a typical electrode material for the anode of commercial lithium-ion batteries, graphite provides a capacity of 372 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at working voltages of 0.1 to 0.2&nbsp;V versus Li/Li<sup>+</sup>. Nevertheless, due to thermodynamic reasons, graphite rarely forms stable interlayer compounds with sodium-ions in carbonate electrolyte system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0002_R_d203940518e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. U.S.A. J. Electrochem. Chem. Rec.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the researchers mainly focused on various amorphous materials with a low degree of graphitization, which includes both hard carbon and soft carbon, is generally regarded as the most promising commercial anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), with sodium storage capacities ≈200–400 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0003_R_d203940518e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is unsatisfactory that most of the superior electrochemical performance of amorphous carbon anodes are obtained at comparatively slow current densities, indicating that the tardiness kinetics of sodium storage. To obtain high current densities performance and long cycle stability of SIBs, there is a need to design a carbon-based anode with satisfactory ion diffusion distance and diffusion coefficient, and rapid electron transfer, which transmits electrons and Na-ions rapidly to the active materials (or active sites).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0004_R_d203940518e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, there are three atomic methods to modify amorphous carbon materials to achieve fast kinetics in SIBs anode, pore structure design, defects introduction, and heteroatom doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0005_R_d203940518e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Small Methods">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Pore-forming is generally assumed that it is a resultful direction to enhance the ion transmits capacity of SIBs and increase the number of sodium storage active sites, but this process first costs more and usually aside with a low initial coulomb efficiency (ICE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0006_R_d203940518e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduction of defects could increase the surface/edge active sites of carbon materials and facilitate the electron transfer, therefore, the electrochemical performance can be further improved.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0007_R_d203940518e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, defects also have some negative effects that cannot be ignored, especially the “capture effect” and the solid electrolyte interface phase (SEI) that produces irreversible capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0008_R_d203940518e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To synthesize carbon materials with specific properties, the physicochemical function­alization of carbon materials is an effective way. It includes embellishment of the carbon <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> lattice by introducing heteroatoms or covalent bonds with particular functional groups or even other molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0009_R_d203940518e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Mater. Chem. A">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This approach offers a possibility to tune the properties of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0002_R_d203940518e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. U.S.A. J. Electrochem. Chem. Rec.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In recent years, the engineering of carbonaceous by heteroatoms (i.e., oxygen (O), boron (B), nitrogen (N), phosphorus (P), and sulfur (S)) doping have been extensively developed, because it can optimize the energy storage properties of carbon materials and improve electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0010_R_d203940518e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J Power Sources J Power Sources Electrochim. Acta Int. J. Hydrogen Energy ACS Sustain. Chem. Eng. Adv. Energy Mater. Nat. Energy">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, O doping is beneficial to increase the layer spacing of carbon skeleton, which was conducive to embedding sodium ions and reset the <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> structure, but the functional groups of oxygen are not stable enough.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0011_R_d203940518e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> B doping can effectively reduce the Fermi level of carbon materials by introducing electronic defects into carbon materials, which contribute to increased capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0012_R_d203940518e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, there is a little research on B doping, which maybe ascribe from the complex and difficult preparation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0013_R_d203940518e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N doping could enhance reactivity, surface wettability, and electronic conductivity by creating non-intrinsic defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0014_R_d203940518e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small Methods">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N atom usually doped at the edges of graphitic layers, but it is also possible for them to be associated with vacancies, thus high N doping is difficult to achieve. And doping N also lower electron conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0015_R_d203940518e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater. Mater. Today">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P atoms can provide an additional capacitive contribution through their strong affinity for alkaline metal ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0016_R_d203940518e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, high P doping is also difficult to achieve. More importantly, sulfur (S) has a larger covalent radius, and doping sulfur atoms can significantly increase the layer spacing of carbon material. Moreover, sulfur-doped carbon material can provide additional active sites for sodium storage and enhance Na-ions diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0017_R_d203940518e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, sulfur doping is a more economical and efficient way to achieve superior performance SIBs anode. In recently reported researches, the bonds species of doped S atoms with carbon skeleton could great affects sodium storing behavior as anodes. Liang et&nbsp;al. doped sulfur atoms in the carbon material to obtain a sodium-ion battery anode material of sulfur-rich N-doped 3D porous carbon framework (SRNDC) with C─S─S─C bond. The results show that the C─S─S─C bond not only increases the carbon layer spacing synergically with the tensile stress on the surface of the material, but also has a strong sodium adsorption capacity and can improve the wettability of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0018_R_d203940518e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, S atoms doped in carbon materials have not only C─S and C─S─S species, but also C═S bonds. In our previous work,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0019_R_d203940518e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C═S group is built on the <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon skeleton to form a three-dimensional penetration structure. The C═S functional group is able to optimize the π-electron conjugation and take shape of space <i>sp</i><sup>2</sup>-hybridized of the carbon skeleton, thus supplying advantageous material properties. Thus, C═S promotes the three-dimensional charge transfer channel of carbon and exhibits rapid reaction kinetics. Although the research of C/S bonds with different bonding effects mentioned above has been studied, however, the synergistic doping of C─S and C═S bonds has not been studied, especially precisely adjusting the ratio of different C/S bonds species. the interaction between dual-type carbon-sulfur bonds and carbon materials need to be further studied, which then provides guidance for improving the electrochemical performance of amorphous carbon.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, considering the superiorities of reasonable structural design and doping, this study reasonably obtained porous hollow carbon nanocages with synergistically doped carbon-sulfur bonds (C─S/C═S) by secondary-sulfidation treatment with H<sub>2</sub>S and carbon disulfide. The C─S/C═S has different C/S bonds formed between the doped sulfur atoms and the carbon skeleton, and its structure is adjusted by secondary-sulfidation to remove other functional groups. Applied to SIBs anodes, C─S/C═S delivered a distinguished reversible capacity of 307 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles, and stable cycle life of 2000 cycles at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Satisfactorily, even at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, C─S/C═S was still capable to gained superior capacity of 186 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The special architecture of C─S/C═S provides a perfect solution, resulting in the excellent sodium storage properties. Through characterization and simulation results, first, C─S/C═S possess interconnected mesoporous carbon structures that promotes the transfer of Na-ions, resulting in superior fast charge capability. Secondly, synergistic doping of C─S and C═S bonds brought about by secondary-sulfidation creates more electrochemical active sites in PHCNCs and improves the electronic conductivity for charge transform. Thirdly, C─S/C═S electrode could accommodate the volume change and provide the fast ion diffusion channel during the period of Na-ions repeated insertion/extraction, ensuring long cycle stability. The results of density functional theory (DFT) calculations show that synergistic doping of C─S and C═S bonds can facilitate electron transfer and increase the adsorb ability of Na-ions, significantly improve the performance of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202400020-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400020-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The different carbon-sulfur bonds doping scheme as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0001">1a</a>. First of all, the precursor PDA@CaCO<sub>3</sub> was formed by self-polymerization of dopamine hydrochloride equably deposit on the surface of nanometer CaCO<sub>3</sub> particles, and then carbonized under Ar atmosphere to obtain PHCNCs@CaCO<sub>3</sub> and a small number of PHCNCs@CaO, the scanning electron microscope (SEM) information and X-ray diffraction (XRD) images of precursor were shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,b</a> (Supporting Information). Carbon dioxide gas is released in the carbonization process, thereby mesoporous pores are formed on the carbon wall. Afterward, the template of calcium oxide and carbonate was removed by etching with hydrochloric acid, as a result, porous hollow carbon nanocages (PHCNCs) are formed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Finally, PHCNCs were doped with secondary sulfur atoms doped method using different sulfur sources, namely H<sub>2</sub>S first and carbon disulfide secondary, and target composites of C─S/C═S with different carbon-sulfur bonds were obtained. Different sulfur sources (H<sub>2</sub>S and CS<sub>2</sub>) have an effect on the type of doping of carbon-sulfur bonds due to the fact that they introduce sulfur atoms with different electron affinities and electron density distributions. This may lead to the formation of different types of bonds during the doping process. The energy level distributions of H<sub>2</sub>S and CS<sub>2</sub> are calculated, and proved the unique role of the nanopore structure. The values of Highest Occupied Molecular Orbital (HUMO) and Lowest Unoccupied Molecular Orbital (LUMO) of two sulfur sources were shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a> (Supporting Information). The larger energy level difference of H<sub>2</sub>S may lead to stronger electron rearrangement effect, which affects the doping type. In contrast, the lower LUMO level of CS<sub>2</sub> shows the high efficiency of C═S modification in carbon materials. In addition, this work distinguishes two stable structural models through computational simulations to explore the mechanism of carbon-sulfur bond doping types, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a> (Supporting Information). The molecular structure of H<sub>2</sub>S and CS<sub>2</sub> sulfur sources determines the types of doped carbon-sulfur bonds, which are carbon-sulfur single bond and carbon-sulfur double bond, respectively. The structural formula of H<sub>2</sub>S is H-S-H, is a polar molecule composed of sulfur atom and two hydrogen atoms, with which hydrogen atoms and sulfur atom form a C─S bond to form the basic structure of H<sub>2</sub>S molecule. CS<sub>2</sub> is a non-polar molecule, consisting of one carbon atom and two sulfur atoms, which share double bonds (C═S) with carbon atoms. The linear structure is simple as S═C═S. The Gibbs free energy of H<sub>2</sub>S (−11.17 KJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) is higher than the Gibbs free energy of CS<sub>2</sub> (−16.66 KJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the C═S produced by CS<sub>2</sub> is more stable than the C─S produced by H<sub>2</sub>S and is not easily broken. As a contrast composite materials, twice H<sub>2</sub>S doped (C─S<sub>(2)</sub>), twice carbon disulfide doped (C═S<sub>(2)</sub>), first carbon disulfide doped and then doped by H<sub>2</sub>S (C═S/C─S), only once H<sub>2</sub>S doped (C─S) and once carbon disulfide doped (C═S) composites were synthesized. The SEM and transmission electron microscope (TEM) images of C─S/C═S (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0001">1b,c</a>), C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a>, Supporting Information), and C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c,d</a>, Supporting Information) indicated that the carbon nanocages are uniformly distributed before and after different sulfidation process, and the inside of the carbon nanocages is hollow. Similarly, the appearance scan information of PHCNCs, C═S/C─S, C─S, and C═S show that the spherical particles appear in dense cross-linked form, but have a loose surface (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), showing that the modification of carbonization and sulfur doping has little or no effect on morphology. Furthermore, calculations are added to compare the structural energy changes before and after doping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The addition of sulfur atoms did not change the stability of the structure, and the structure energy after doping was not much different from that before doping. This shows that sulfur doping, especially the C─S/C═S bond, does not cause significant changes in the structure of the system, indicating that the sulfur doping process does not affect the stability of the structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400020-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/198593b4-cabe-492a-aa46-05a830c7b09a/adfm202400020-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/198593b4-cabe-492a-aa46-05a830c7b09a/adfm202400020-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a8d2d875-0a33-460c-86de-32fb2604c320/adfm202400020-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/198593b4-cabe-492a-aa46-05a830c7b09a/adfm202400020-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400020-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400020" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of material synthesis. The illustration is 2D cross-section of model diagram of a six-membered ring carbon materials. b) SEM, c) TEM, and d) HRTEM images of C─S/C═S. e) Elemental distribution (C, N, and S) by the EDS analysis of C─S/C═S.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In the high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image of C─S/C═S (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0001">1d</a>), the wall thickness is about 8–10&nbsp;nm. In addition, the microstructure of C─S/C═S material is chaotic and disordered. The TEM energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) mappings of C─S/C═S confirm the well-distributed of C, S, and N (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0001">1e</a>). According to the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) curve of PHCNCs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), carbon material has a loss of mass between 400 and 500 °C, indicated the decomposition of carbon react with oxygen. And a high weight loss rate of the carbon material near 100%, revealed the complete removal of the calcium carbonate template and the purity of the synthesized carbon material. The nitrogen adsorption/desorption testing with Brunauer-Emmette-Teller (BET) theory were mainly used to test surface area and pore information of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, and PHCNCs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The surface areas of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs are 47.23, 46.70, 48.47, and 47.68 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), respectively. Their pore size distributions (PSDs) are analyzed by Density Functional Theory (DFT) model in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a> (Supporting Information) clearly exhibit two sets of mesopores. The primary pore size is centered at 3&nbsp;nm, these are the pores created by doping of carbon-sulfur bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0020_R_d203940518e774" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The second pore size is located at 6–8&nbsp;nm, which is obtained by carbonization and hydrochloric acid etching of PHCNCs@CaCO<sub>3</sub>. The porous structure could ensure adequate and effective electrode/electrolyte contact, which is more conducive to the electrochemical reaction inside the battery. The C─S/C═S interconnected mesoporous structure ensures fast electron transfer and ion diffusion length. These characteristics are more conducive to faster reaction kinetics of the electrode at high current density. It is further proved that the synergistic doping of the dure-type carbon-sulfur bonds (C─S/C═S) can improve the pore size distribution of carbon materials.</p>
         
         <p>Firstly, Raman spectroscopy was used to investigate the defects in the graphite plane of the synthesized carbon material. Exhibited in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), two strong Raman characteristic peaks of carbon atom crystal at ≈1350 and 1560 cm<sup>−1</sup> are disordered carbon (D band) and crystalline graphite carbon (G band), respectively. Specifically speaking, D is the defect, representing the information of the defect, G is the graphite, indicating the in-plane tensile vibration of the carbon atom <i>sp<sup>2</sup></i> hybrid. The peak intensity ratio of D and G band is the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value, which represents the degree of unordered of carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0001_R_d203940518e804" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater. Small Stru. Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The value of I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> is 1.03 for C─S/C═S, which is higher than C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (0.935), C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (0.915), PHCNCs (0.91), C═S/C─S (0.93), C─S (1) and C═S (0.98) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), the high value of C─S/C═S could be attributed to the heat treatment of secondary-sulfidation increases their graphite degree, and the doping of sulfur increases their defect degree. In addition, the G-band of C─S/C═S (1576 cm<sup>−1</sup>) has a redshift compared with PHCNCs (1587 cm<sup>−1</sup>), demonstrating that there are holes, vacancies, and edges in the C─S/C═S skeleton.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0021_R_d203940518e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These can work as active sites for sodium storage in carbon anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0022_R_d203940518e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2b</a> analyzes the projected crystal orbital Hamilton population (pCOHP) of C─S and C═S bonds. The integral value of pCOHP (iCOHP) can reflect the bonding strength.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0019_R_d203940518e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Environ. Sci.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The weak C─S bond intensity (−iCOHP value is 1.9) manifested that the C─S bonds in carbon material are more likely to broken that could provide more active sites but not benefit the stability of the carbon material. And C═S bonds (−iCOHP value is 2.1) are more stable, conducive to creates stable structure in PHCNCs and improves the electrochemical activity and electronic conductivity. Thus, C─S/C═S bonds synergistically doped could built a more active and stable carbon-based anode for SIBs. The change of interlayer spacing information was shown by X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). All curves have two wide peaks of amorphous carbon, 002 and 100. Where the 2<i>θ</i> degree of (002) peak of C─S/C═S is located at 23.2°, on the basis of Bragg equation, the calculated (002) layer spacing is 0.383&nbsp;nm. Compared with PHCNCs (22.6°, 0.362&nbsp;nm), C─S/C═S has an increased layer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0023_R_d203940518e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Deeply, the C═S bond doped could more enlarge the layer spacing than C─S compared with C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (23.0°, 0.373&nbsp;nm), C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (24.6°, 0.392&nbsp;nm), C═S/C─S (23.5°, 0.378&nbsp;nm). And secondary-sulfidation could optimize the functional groups in carbon skeleton, compared with C─S (23.5°, 0.378&nbsp;nm) and C═S (22.0°, 0.403&nbsp;nm). Thus, the most suitable layer spacing of C─S/C═S could not only provide more active sites by S doping, but also maintain a durable structure while sodium storing. Notably, all samples doped with sulfur were tested by elemental analysis and showed approximate sulfur atomic content. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), which consistent with the effect of increasing carbon layer spacing caused by sulfur doping.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0017_R_d203940518e869" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it is obviously noted that the carbon layer spacing of secondary-sulfidation (C─S<sub>(2)</sub> and C═S<sub>(2)</sub>) is smaller than that of primary sulfidation (C─S and C═S) in the same sulfur source (H<sub>2</sub>S and carbon disulfide). There are two reasons for this phenomenon. First of all, during the secondary-sulfidation, the degree of doping increases, but it is also accompanied the evaporation of sulfur atoms causes tensile stress on the appearance of the carbon material, causes the shrinkage of nanoparticles. This is not conducive to the synergistic effect between the internal stress and doping of the material. The second reason is that the sulfidation temperature is as high as 550&nbsp;°C, when sulfur atoms are doped, carbon materials are also carbonized. The secondary carbonization can optimize the local structure of the chaotic nanodomains and homogenize the layer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0018_R_d203940518e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, after secondary-sulfidation, the carbon layer spacing will be reduced. In addition, since the C═S bond is more stable than the C─S bond (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2b</a>), the use of carbon disulfide sulfidation to doping the C═S bond reduces the evaporation of sulfur atoms and alleviates the shrinkage of nanoparticles, resulting in only a slight reduction in the carbon layer spacing. Therefore, the carbon layer spacing of C─S/C═S is greater than C═S/C─S.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400020-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/61077f71-df68-4008-b539-0e4f765a611a/adfm202400020-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/61077f71-df68-4008-b539-0e4f765a611a/adfm202400020-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aaedcbdd-1989-4b9d-9371-88fcad46cc1b/adfm202400020-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/61077f71-df68-4008-b539-0e4f765a611a/adfm202400020-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400020-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400020" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman scattering spectra of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2),</sub> and PHCNCs. b) COHP analysis of the C─S and C═S bonds. c) X-ray diffraction patterns of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs. d) Elemental analysis of sulfur atomic content of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S/C─S, C─S and C═S. e) X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy spectra of S 2<i>p</i> of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub> and C═S<sub>(2)</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Given the above considerations, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) confirms the existence, valence states, and bonding of S and C. In the XPS full spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), S and C were shown to be present in C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S/C─S, C─S, and C═S. The high-resolution fine spectra of S 2<i>p</i> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information) are fitted into distinct peaks. Among them, C─S/C═S, the two kinds of peaks at ≈164.3 and 163.3&nbsp;eV are ascribed to S 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and S 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> of the C─S bonds, meanwhile, another two kind of peaks at ≈162.8 and 161.9&nbsp;eV are ascribed to S 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and S 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> of the C═S bonds. Where the weak peak of high binding energy corresponds to sulfate (RSO<i><sub>x</sub></i>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0024_R_d203940518e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another peak at 160.9&nbsp;eV and 159.8&nbsp;eV can be ascribed to the C─SH accessory substance, which was produced during sulfidation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0025_R_d203940518e969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks of C═S 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> and C═S 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> are not present in C─S<sub>(2)</sub> treated with H<sub>2</sub>S. Similarly, there are five peaks in the high-resolution fine spectra of C 1<i>s</i> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12b,c</a>, Supporting Information), located at 284.6, 285.3, 286.3, 287.8, and 289.1&nbsp;eV, attributed to C─C, C─S, C─N/C─O, C═S and C═O bonds, respectively. The above analysis proves that secondary-sulfidation successfully doped C─S and C═S bonds in porous hollow carbon nanocages. The atomic percentages obtained by elemental analysis (EA) of C─S/C═S and other five composites, displayed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Combined with the high-resolution fine spectra of S 2<i>p</i>, the peak areas of the different carbon-sulfur bonds are integrated and expressed them as percentages in the sulfur atom content histogram (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). In order to distinguish the influence of nitrogen content in polydopamine precursors on C─S/C═S, the N1<i>s</i> high resolution-spectra of XPS of four samples of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2),</sub> and PHCNCs are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information). The peak intensity of pyrrolic-N and pyridinic-N is obviously different from the peak fitting results. When undoped, the peak of pyrrolic-N of PHCNCs is stronger than that of pyridinic-N (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13d</a>, Supporting Information). When secondary vulcanization is performed, the peak intensity of pyridine-N increased (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a–c</a>, Supporting Information), with C─S/C═S at 42.10%, C─S<sub>(2)</sub> at 39.95%, and C═S<sub>(2)</sub> at 34.06%, respectively. Therefore, it can be concluded that potential secondary carbonization during secondary sulfidation helps to increase the strength of pyridine-N, thereby improving the electronic conductivity of PHCNCs, and improving its electrochemical performance. The composite C─S/C═S delivered the similar bonding content of C─S and C═S, synergistically C─S and C═S bonds doped not only supply more active sites, but also maintain the stability of the carbon structure with a high doped sulfur content. The suitable content of different sulfur species could distinguish and provide a good direction for developing superior SIBs anodes.</p>
         
         <p>To further investigate the introduction of different carbon-sulfur bonds (C─S and C═S) by secondary-sulfidation to optimize carbon nanocages to improve sodium storage performance, the C─S/C═S and other electrodes were assembled into half-cell to measure the electrochemical performance. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) testing to observe electrochemical behavior within the potential window. <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) show the original five CV curves of seven electrodes in 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3a</a>, The CV curves of C─S/C═S show the “adsorption, insertion, and filling” behavior of Na-ions. Specifically, the capacity of the high potential platform (&gt;0.1&nbsp;V) is caused by the “adsorption” and “insertion” of the active sites and carbon layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0023_R_d203940518e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0026_R_d203940518e1043" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Commun.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the capacity of the low potential platform (&lt;0.1&nbsp;V) is caused by “pore filling”, including open and closed pores. CV curves shown two pair of redox peaks at potentials of 1.56/1.73 and 1.20/2.10&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na with high degree of overlap curves of the initial five loop curves, revealing the electrochemical stability of C─S/C═S. In particular, the initial curve was larger than the posterior curves, which was attributed to the irreversible side reaction between electrolyte and C─S/C═S, and simultaneously the formation of SEI.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400020-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9254eb48-f376-4acd-8289-979201ba6ec1/adfm202400020-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9254eb48-f376-4acd-8289-979201ba6ec1/adfm202400020-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c2f03e2a-ee5e-4eee-bc4d-17e5a02c5f70/adfm202400020-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9254eb48-f376-4acd-8289-979201ba6ec1/adfm202400020-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400020-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400020" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical characterization of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2),</sub> and PHCNCs as the half-cell anode in 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. a) The initial five CV curves of C─S/C═S at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Cycle test of the electrodes at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves (1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) at the 1st, 2nd, 10th, 50th, and 100th cycles of C─S/C═S. d) ICE value at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Ultralong cycle of C─S/C═S. f) Rate performance of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs. g) The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of C─S/C═S anodes from 0.1 to 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. h) The comparison of rate properties for C─S/C═S with various recently reported high-performance carbon electrode materials.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The cycling performance of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, PHCNCs, C═S/C─S, C─S and C═S were explored at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3b</a>, the capacity retention of all samples is nearly 100% after 200 cycles, further demonstrating superior electrochemical stability. This is due to the interconnected mesoporous carbon skeleton, which is able to adapt to the volume change when Na-ions are repeatedly inserted/extracted, and provides a fast ion diffusion channel, guaranteeing a stable cycle life. However, the specific capacity of C─S/C═S reach up to 307 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is outclass than PHCNCs (94.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (207.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3b,c</a>). It is proved that the synergistically-doped carbon-sulfur bonds (C─S, C═S) brought about by secondary-sulfidation creates more electrochemically active sites in PHCNCs, improves the electronic conductivity of charge conversion, promotes electron transfer, increases the adsorption capacity of Na-ions, and significantly improves the storage of Na-ions. Since the C═S bond is more stable than the C─S bond, and more C═S doped could adjust the carbon skeleton and stimulate more active sites from doped C─S and other intrinsic defects. The C═S and C─S bond in C─S/C═S has a stronger bonding synergistic effect than C═S/C─S, resulting in a higher specific capacity of C─S/C═S than C═S/C─S (259 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). Compared with single sulfidation, C─S (201 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and C═S (239 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), the C─S/C═S obtained by secondary-sulfidation also showed a higher specific capacity value, which further revealed the favorable effect of synergistically-doped carbon-sulfur bonds carbon anode on electrochemical sodium storage (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information) show the charge/discharge curves at the 1st, 2nd, 10th, 50th, 100th, 200th, and 500th cycles of C─S/C═S at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The curves of the initial cycle of C─S/C═S do not overlap well and with some variations. That's because the initial test is accompanied by the formation of SEI film, and carbon material activation, including the activation of defects, carbon layer spacing, and pore structure, which is a common phenomenon in carbon anodes. Therefore, there will be some irreversible reaction generation, so it is significantly different from the initial cycle. In order to further prove the above analysis, the charge/discharge curves of 200 cycles and 500 cycles were selected for comparative analysis, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). These curves were nearly overlapping, indicating that the C─S/C═S anode reaction was closer to stable in the late cycle period. Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16d–f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information) show the curves for the 1st, 2nd, 10th, 50th, and 100th cycles of, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, PHCNCs, C═S/C─S, C─S and C═S at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. PHCNCs electrodes have very poor capacity retention, and the cycle curves of the C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S/C─S, C─S and C═S both have two platforms, one less than 1.0&nbsp;V and one greater than 1.0&nbsp;V. The energy density of the full-cell is closely related to the voltage window, therefore, the discharge capacity under 1.0&nbsp;V is more critical. Calculating the specific capacity under 1.0&nbsp;V, the capacity of C─S/C═S is 168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, the highest of all samples, which means that C─S/C═S exhibited more suitable working potential as anodes than other samples in this study. The specific capacity under 1.0&nbsp;V attributed to the adsorption, insertion, and mesoporous filling of Na-ions in the material surface. The ability to adsorb Na-ions up to the active site and surface area. Especially, C─S/C═S have a relatively low specific surface area, so the active site is higher, which further proves that the introduction of C─S bond and C═S bond modifies the carbon material, increases the active site of the material. The initial coulomb efficiency (ICE) of C─S/C═S after secondary-sulfidation of different sulfur sources was 80.15%, which was not only superior to the C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (70.42%), C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (65.83%), PHCNCs (69.27%), C═S/C─S (72.23%), C─S (73.38%) and C═S (75.01%), as show in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), but also more outstanding than that of other composite material in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0017_R_d203940518e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett.">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0018_R_d203940518e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0022_R_d203940518e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0027_R_d203940518e1171" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano App. Surf. Sci.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Because of after secondary-sulfidation, C─S/C═S have suitable defects and active sites on the surface of carbon material on account of the synergistic effect of C─S and C═S bonds, particularly, the C═S bond, which reduces the irreversible capture of Na-ions during charge and discharge, that is, the “capture effect”,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0008_R_d203940518e1176" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> thereby reducing the loss of initial irreversible capacity. Even cycling at 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, C─S/C═S can still deliver a capacity of 223 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and 182 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained after 2000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3e</a>). This further confirmed that the C─S/C═S electrode can accommodate the volume variation when Na-ions are repeatedly inserted/extracted due to the synergistically-doped carbon-sulfur bonds in the inter-porous hollow carbon skeleton material, and provide a fast ion diffusion channel to ensure a long cycle life.</p>
         
         <p>Compared to C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, and PHCNCs, C─S/C═S anodes exhibits high rate performance from 0.1 to 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3f,g</a>). Make a detailed analysis, specific capacities of 346, 338, 317, 297, 279, 254, and 220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are acquired for the corresponding current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Though at the uppermost current density of 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the C─S/C═S also delivered a satisfactory specific capacity of 186 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Compared to C═S/C─S (153 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), C─S (132 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and C═S (121 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) anodes at the similar testing condition (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), C─S/C═S anode also exhibits obvious fast charging advantages. Otherwise, the comparison of rate capability with reported researches on carbon-based anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3h</a>) further proved that the reaction kinetics of C─S/C═S composite structure better than the most of reported works.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0027_R_d203940518e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Nano App. Surf. Sci.">27</a>, <a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0028_R_d203940518e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Sci. Adv. Sci. Carbon ChemSusChem Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information), the influence of sulfur doping on nitrogen in precursor PHCNCs material was analyzed, and the conclusion was drawn that the secondary sulfidation process could increase the content of pyridine nitrogen and improve the electronic conductivity of carbon material, thus improving the electrochemical performance of PHCNCs. In order to distinguish whether the improved electrochemical performance of PHCNCs is caused by sulfur doping rather than secondary carbonization process at the same time of secondary sulfidation, the precursor of PHCNCs is secondary carbonized according to the temperature conditions of sulfidation (PHCNCs<sub>(2)</sub>). The cycling performance of PHCNCs<sub>(2)</sub> were explored at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a</a>, Supporting Information). After two carbonizations, the cycling performance of PHCNCs is improved, from 98 to 139 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is lower than the 307 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of C─S/C═S, and the rate performance is also improved, from 10 to 40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under the condition of high current density 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which is also much lower than the 186 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of C─S/C═S (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b</a>, Supporting Information). These results further prove that the excellent electrochemical performance of C─S/C═S is dominated by the synergistically-doped of C─S bond and C═S bond, and the increase of pyridine nitrogen content is only a slight improvement rather than the main factor.</p>
         
         <p>With the same doping method, our supplementary experiment selected carbon tube as the precursor system to prepare carbon tube (C─S/C═S) in order to compare with the porous hollow carbon nanocages treated under different sulfur sources. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images at different magnifications (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a,b</a>, Supporting Information) confirmed that we have synthesized nanoscale carbon tubes and that the same doping steps do not change the structure of the Carbon tube. Then the electrochemical properties of carbon tube and carbon tube (C─S/C═S), i.e., cycling performance and rate performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information) were tested. Compared to PHCNCs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3b</a>), the specific capacity of the carbon tube (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22a</a>, Supporting Information) (180 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) is almost twice that of the PHCNCs (95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). However, after secondary sulfidation with different sulfur sources, the specific capacity of carbon tube (C─S/C═S) is only increased by about 40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, reaching 220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and PHCNCs (C─S/C═S) is increased by ≈200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> to 307 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Similarly, rate performance tests show that the capacity of carbon tubes (C─S/C═S) at high current densities (20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) is not significantly improved (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22b</a>, Supporting Information, 101 to 125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), while PHCNCs (C─S/C═S) is increased from 10 to 186 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3f</a>). These results indicate that this method of introducing C─S bonds and C═S bonds to improve the electrochemical properties of carbon materials is also effective for other precursor, such as carbon tubes, although its improvement performance is not as good as that of PHCNCs. In order to explore this difference in electrochemical properties, high-resolution fine spectral analysis of S 2<i>p</i> of carbon tubes (C─S/C═S) is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a</a> (Supporting Information). The fitting results confirmed that, similar to PHCNCs (C─S/C═S), the carbon tube (C─S/C═S) was successfully doped with C─S and C═S bonds for secondary sulfidation of different sulfur sources. The atomic percentages obtained by elemental analysis (EA) of Carbon tube (C─S/C═S). The atomic percentages of C, N, O, and S are 83.92%, 5.43%, 7.19%, and 3.46%, respectively. Combined with the high-resolution fine spectra of S 2<i>p</i>, the peak areas of the different carbon-sulfur bonds are integrated, where C═S, C─S, C─SH, and sulfate account for 25.10%, 25.19%, 24.68%, and 25.03%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23b</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with PHCNCs (C─S/C═S) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0002">2d</a>), the doped sulfur content of carbon tube is only 3.46%, and the content proportion of C─S bond and C═S bond is also lower than that of PHCNCs (C─S/C═S). Therefore, the doped sulfur content, especially the introduction of C─S bonds and C═S bonds, is the key to the electrochemical performance of carbon tube and PHCNCs.</p>
         
         <p>In order to further investigate the fast kinetics of C─S/C═S anode, the Nyquist plots of four electrodes are reflected in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information), which consist of a semicircle of the high-frequency region and a slash of the low frequency region. R<sub>SEI</sub> (the resistance of sodium ions to pass through the SEI membrane) and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (the adsorption capacity of the interface to ions plus the ability of charge transfer to cause electrochemical reactions of ions at the interface) of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, PHCNCs, C═S/C─S, C─S, and C═S were obtained by fitting the Nyquist curve in the high-frequency area. The fitted R<sub>SEI</sub> value of the C─S/C═S electrode was 6.51&nbsp;Ohm before cycling and 1.69&nbsp;Ohm after 10 cycles, which was reduced by 74.05% and higher than that of C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (40.20%), C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (14.82%), PHCNCs (−75.51%, indicates an increase in impedance after cycling), C═S/C─S (61.97%), C─S (60.59%) and C═S (47.09%). This proves that during the battery cycles, the impedance values of other electrodes sodium ions through SEI greatly reduced except PHCNCs, and C─S/C═S electrodes is particularly obvious, thus further proved that the synergistically-doped carbon-sulfur bonds and their synergy are more conducive to forming a good SEI. Besides, the fitted <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of the C─S/C═S electrode was 9.90&nbsp;Ohm before cycling and 1.15&nbsp;Ohm after 10 cycles, which was reduced by 88.35% and higher than that of C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (71.09%), C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (0.02%), PHCNCs (78.92%), C═S/C─S (87.85%), C─S (86.46%) and C═S (55.98%), which proves that the synergistically-doped carbon-sulfur bonds electrode (C─S/C═S) surface has a better reaction rate. In particular, the detailed fit information was shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24a–c</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). Furthermore, the C─S/C═S anode with C─S and C═S bonds presents the minimum slope in Warburg area (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">4b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24d</a>, Supporting Information), indicating rapid solid-state ion transport in the active component. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) is generally used to obtain diffusion coefficient by electrochemical measurement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0029_R_d203940518e1365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Commun.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The working principle of GITT is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4c</a>. Then, the Na-ions diffusion coefficients were calculated and the consequence were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4d</a>. Definitely, the C─S/C═S has the most superior ion diffusion coefficient in comparison with the C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs, which illustrates that porous hollow carbon nanocages and synergistically-doped C─S and C═S bonds in C─S/C═S could greatly accelerate the migration of Na-ions within the active material.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400020-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd53313b-5198-4e22-8da5-90365504b061/adfm202400020-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd53313b-5198-4e22-8da5-90365504b061/adfm202400020-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/69273289-1cbe-4a94-8fc6-8d987672734d/adfm202400020-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd53313b-5198-4e22-8da5-90365504b061/adfm202400020-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400020-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400020" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Nyquist plots of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub>, and PHCNCs after 10 cycles and b) the correlation linear fitting in low-frequency region. c,d) The GITT test calculates the ion diffusion coefficient as well as the voltage situation. e) The first CV curve at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> scan rate of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs. f,g) CV results of C─S/C═S from 0.1 to 5.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and the determined <i>b</i>-value was calculated by peak current and scan rate. h) Pseudocapacitive and diffusive contribution at 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> of C─S/C═S. i) Normalized contribution to capacitance and diffusion of C─S/C═S at different scan rates.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The assembled half cells were subjected to CV tests with a scanning rate from 0.1 to 5.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4e,f</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) to further investigate the electrochemical reaction and kinetic mechanism. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4f</a> depicts the sweep CV of C─S/C═S. Distinctly, the redox peak increased with the increasing scan rate in the course of the insertion/extraction of Na-ions. According to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4g</a>, the <i>b</i> values calculated by the Log (Peak current) versus Log (scan rate) plots for reduction peaks (R1 and R2) and oxidation peaks (O1 and O2) are 0.51, 0.97, 0.67, and 0.96, respectively. Generally, the <i>b</i> value corresponding to the charge transfer mechanism. The <i>b</i> value close to 1 indicates that charge transfer in C─S/C═S composites is dominated by interfacial capacitance behavior, which ensures rapid sodium storage kinetics. Further revealing the preponderance structure design of porous hollow carbon nanocages active substance and synergistically-doped C─S and C═S bonds increasing ion diffusion rate in C─S/C═S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0001_R_d203940518e1442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater. Small Stru. Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To quantitatively examine the contribution of each process, the relationship between potential <i>v</i> and its current <i>i</i> as follows: <i>i (V) = k<sub>1</sub>v+k<sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup></i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0030_R_d203940518e1461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Nano">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pseudo-capacitance contributions of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs could be obtained by calculating the <i>k<sub>1</sub></i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub></i> constants (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4h</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26a–f</a>, Supporting Information), which are 78.9%, 78.6%, 79.4% and 41.9% at 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and the potential distribution diagram corresponding to the pseudocapacitive current and the measured current are also demonstrated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0031_R_d203940518e1488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nanoscale">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the pseudocapacitive participation of the C─S/C═S electrode expands to 91.9% at 5.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0004">4i</a>). There is no doubt that a higher percentage of pseudocapacitance contribution can accelerate faster Na-ions transport and enhance the reaction kinetics of the C─S/C═S, which benefit from the conductive porous hollow carbon nanocages and synergistically-doped C─S and C═S bonds.</p>
         
         <p>In order to further study the structural changes of C─S/C═S during sodium storage, the in-suit Raman spectrum of C─S/C═S during sodiation/desodiation process was presented. The D and G bands of C─S/C═S in the Raman spectra changed in the different sodiation/desodiation states were observed (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0005">5a</a>). In the second stable electrochemical cycle test, the discharge curve ranges from 1.8 to 0.01&nbsp;V, the location of D and G bands occurs a left shift accompanied by the reduced peak intensity. Meanwhile, the Na-ions surface adsorption, insertion, and pore filling in SIBs also occur at this stage. In the charging process from 0.01 to 3.0&nbsp;V, the D and G bands intensity increase by degrees, and the position of D bands and G bands displays a right shift, which is attributed to the process of desodiation, indicating that the structure of C─S/C═S return to the original state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202400020-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202400020-bib-0032_R_d203940518e1507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Carbon Energy">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the same time, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values calculated from the D-peak and G-peak areas also changed during the potential change, from 1.07 of the initial discharge potential of 1.80&nbsp;V to around 0.77 at the end of discharge, eventually returns to 1.03 when charged to 3.0&nbsp;V, we can observe a location movement of D and G band, indicating that the structure of C─S/C═S gradually return to the original state after the desodiation process. Therefore, the durable and superior kinetic sodium ion storage performance of C─S/C═S carbon skeleton anode was ascribed to the synergistic effect. The synergistic effect of C─S/C═S doped (C─S could break bond and react with sodium and improved the capacity by non-Faraday reaction, while C═S could maintain the stability of carbon skeleton and C═S modification of structure provide more active sites of carbon skeleton while storage) have been indicated by the ex-situ XPS and ex-situ XRD results. Firstly, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0005">5b</a> and with the peak potential of CV curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0003">3a</a> as reference, high-resolution fine spectrum analysis of S 2<i>p</i> was performed on the initial state of the electrodes and the electrodes that were discharged to 1.56, 1.20, and 0.01&nbsp;V, charged to 1.73, 2.10, and 3.00&nbsp;V. It can be clearly seen that C═S<sub>3/2</sub> and C═S<sub>1/2</sub> near 160.8 and 161.9&nbsp;eV shift toward high binding energy at 0.01&nbsp;V discharge to 161.7 and 162.5&nbsp;eV respectively, however, when continuing to charge to 3.00&nbsp;V to complete a cycle, the peaks of the two C═S bonds return to their original positions around 160.8&nbsp;eV and 161.9&nbsp;eV. Notably, C─S<sub>3/2</sub> and C─S<sub>1/2</sub> located near 163.5 and 164.6&nbsp;eV are irreversibly shifted towards lower binding energies until the end of the cycle, the positions are shifted to 162.8 and 163.9&nbsp;eV, respectively. The result revealed the react of C─S bond with sodium. Moreover, combined with the high-resolution fine spectra of S 2<i>p</i>, the peak areas of the different carbon-sulfur bonds in different states during operation are integrated. As shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), when the discharge reaches 0.01&nbsp;V, the proportion of C─S peak area changes significantly, decreasing from 25.26% to 21.85%, while the proportion of C─S peak area increases when charge to 3.0&nbsp;V, indicating that C─S bond was react with sodium and this is a reversible reaction. Notably, the contents of C═S bond near have no change, which indicated the C═S was not react with sodium.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400020-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d41b771a-5813-46b8-9ac9-77075cbf4d28/adfm202400020-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d41b771a-5813-46b8-9ac9-77075cbf4d28/adfm202400020-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3455679f-75da-486d-91ac-ed9a119d0709/adfm202400020-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d41b771a-5813-46b8-9ac9-77075cbf4d28/adfm202400020-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400020-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400020" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ Raman spectrum of C─S/C═S and electrochemical curves (second electrochemical cycle) of corresponding potentials and I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> values of corresponding potentials. b) The ex-situ XPS high-resolution resolution-spectra of S 2<i>p</i> of C─S/C═S. c) Simulations were conducted to investigate the differential charge densities of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2),</sub> and PHCNCs, yellow is the electron accumulation area, cyan is the electron loss area. d) Theoretical simulations of Na-ions diffusion paths for the three models. (e) Theoretical simulations of sodium-ion diffusion energy barriers along ion diffusion path of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2),</sub> and PHCNCs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In additionally, the ex-situ XRD results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information) further distinguished the specify react of C─S/C═S with sodium and the changes of Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub>, NaC<sub>x,</sub> and sulfate compounds during the operation of the C─S/C═S electrode. during the operation of the C─S/C═S electrode. From the initial state to the discharge of 1.56&nbsp;V, the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> 30.40°, 30.80°, 24.02°, 23.73°, and 23.34° could be clearly observed, at the same time, a small peak of Na<sub>2</sub>S appeared at 36.63°, indicating the C─S bond breaks, a large amount of sulfur reacts with a small amount of sodium ions to produce primarily Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub>, along with a small amount of Na<sub>2</sub>S, as do sulfates and carbides. Further discharge to 1.20&nbsp;V the peak of Na<sub>2</sub>S appeared more obvious indicated the continuous reaction of C─S with sodium.
