This phenomenon and trend continue until discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, at which time the relative proportion of C─S peak area reaches the minimum, while the relative proportion of C═S peak area has hardly changed, which further explains the instability of C─S bond compared with C═S bond. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information), at 0.01&nbsp;V, corresponding peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S, NaC<sub>x,</sub> and sulfate produced by the reaction reach the highest intensity, at which time the maximum capacity of sodium storage is reached, then gradually decreasing during the process of charging and completing a complete cycle. Corresponding to Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0005">5b</a>, after completing a whole cycle (charging to 3.00&nbsp;V), the C═S fitting peak can be observed to be slightly enhanced compared with the initial state, but the C─S fitting peak becomes weaker, which can also be seen from Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The C═S bond is reversible and can stabilize the structure of C─S/C═S, thereby improving the stability of the electrode reaction. This result indicated the synergistic effect of C─S and C═S doped (C─S could break bond and react with sodium and improved the capacity by non-Faraday reaction, while C═S could maintain the stability of carbon skeleton and C═S modification of structure provide more active sites of carbon skeleton while storage) could guarantee the high capacity and long cycle stability while storage sodium.</p>
         
         <p>To provide additional insight into the experimental findings, this study carried out a comprehensive theoretical investigation into the Na-ions adsorption capacity of C─S bond and C═S bond synergistically modify porous hollow carbon nanocages structure utilizing density functional theory (DFT). Simulations were conducted to explore the differential charge densities (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0005">5c</a>) in simplified model of graphene skeleton of C─S/C═S, C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2),</sub> and PHCNCs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28a</a>, Supporting Information), yellow is the electron accumulation area, cyan is the electron loss area. It can be discerned that notable fluctuations in electron density exist across the distinct yellow and cyan regions. These variations suggest that there are discernible differences in the electron distribution within the various systems under study. In all instances, there is a remarkably aggrandize in the charge density within the intermediate zone connecting the Na-ions and the carbon layer. This indicates that the charge is transferred from the adsorbed Na-ions to the closest carbon atom. This phenomenon was more pronounced in C─S/C═S compared to C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs. The above shows the C─S/C═S exhibit higher affinity for Na-ions adsorption and more charge accumulation around the C─S and C═S bonds sites, corroborating the experimental results. To get further understand the superior electrochemical properties of the C─S/C═S, the migration routes of Na-ions and the associated energy barriers along these pathways were calculated using the CL-NEB method (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0005">5d,e</a>). The C─S/C═S exhibit lower Na-ion diffusion energy barriers and the value is 0.13&nbsp;eV, compared to the diffusion energy barrier in C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (0.20 eV), C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (0.21 eV) and PHCNCs (0.35 eV) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). Most remarkably, the diffusion energy barriers of C─S<sub>(2)</sub> were almost identical with C═S<sub>(2)</sub>. This is consistent with the conclusion of other tests above, namely that carbon-sulfur bond-modified porous hollow carbon nanocages produce a low diffusion energy barrier. And to analyze the Na-ion adsorption capacity of each model, a solitary Na atom was positioned at various locations within each system, and the resulting geometry was subsequently optimized through calculation (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28c–f</a>, Supporting Information). The adsorption energy (<i>E<sub>a</sub></i>) of each structure for Na ions was then calculated. The calculations revealed the <i>E<sub>a</sub></i> was C─S/C═S (<i>E<sub>a</sub></i> = −3.86&nbsp;eV), C─S<sub>(2)</sub> (<i>E<sub>a</sub></i> = −3.47&nbsp;eV), C═S<sub>(2)</sub> (<i>E<sub>a</sub></i> = −3.43&nbsp;eV) and PHCNCs (<i>E<sub>a</sub></i> = 3.32&nbsp;eV), indicating that the C─S/C═S anodes has a stronger adsorption energy, further suggest that the synergy of C─S and C═S bond possess considerably greater Na-ion adsorption capacity. In conclusion, the unique synergistic doping of C─S and C═S bonds of C─S/C═S result in the higher adsorption energy for Na-ion as well as fast charge transfer compared to C─S<sub>(2)</sub>, C═S<sub>(2)</sub> and PHCNCs, which further contribute to its superior electrochemical properties. These discoveries offer valuable understanding into the source of the observed exceptional electrochemical characteristics exhibited by these materials.</p>
         
         <p>Full-cells were assembled to further verify the practical application of C─S/C═S. The schematic illustration of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || C─S/C═S was displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0006">6a</a>. The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information) cathode exhibits a pair of sharp redox peaks in CV curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0006">6b</a>), corresponds to the charge/discharge plateaus at 3.25&nbsp;V with high capacity of 105 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1C (1C = 110&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information), the cycle curve of half-cell assembled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode and sodium metal reveals the wide operating voltage of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and the specific capacity close to 70.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0006">6c</a> displays that the assembled full cell of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || C─S/C═S has a capacity of 73 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles at 1C. At the same time, commercial hard carbon was selected as a comparison, and its specific capacity was only 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles. The inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0006">6c</a> shows that the assembled full-cell can power a series light–emitting diode (LED) bulbs on account of high output potential (near 3.25&nbsp;V, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information). There is no significant difference in rate performance at low current density (0.1C), but at high current density, especially 5C, the capacity of the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || Harde carbon decays very severe (10 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || C─S/C═S maintains a capacity of 53 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0006">6d</a>). In particular, the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || C─S/C═S full battery also has a good capacity after a long cycle, which still has 46 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202400020-fig-0006">6e</a>), which proved that it has great prospect in the practical application of SIBs</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202400020-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e42f55c9-b8a1-4baa-ab65-a58deaccd37f/adfm202400020-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e42f55c9-b8a1-4baa-ab65-a58deaccd37f/adfm202400020-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c31c864a-98d7-4691-9e3c-405de2af0c26/adfm202400020-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e42f55c9-b8a1-4baa-ab65-a58deaccd37f/adfm202400020-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202400020-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202400020" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The electrochemical characterization of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as the half-cell cathode in 2.0 − 4.0&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. a) Schematic of full-cell with C─S/C═S anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. b) CV curves of C─S/C═S and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Cycle test comparison of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>||C─S/C═S and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || Hard carbon full-cell at 1C (1C = 110&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). d) Rate test comparison of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || C─S/C═S and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || Hard carbon full-cell at the discharge current density of 1 C. e) long-term cyclic performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> || C─S/C═S full-cell at the charging current density of 2C and the discharge current density of 1C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202400020-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400020-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we developed synergistically-doped C─S and C═S bonds modify porous hollow carbon nanocages material (C─S/C═S) and exhibit superior Na-ions storage behaviors with an excellent ICE, stable cycle life of 2000 cycles, and a fast-charging ability of 186 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Base on the analyses of experimental and theoretical calculations, the durable and fast Na-ions storage performance result from the unique C─S and C═S bonds synergistically doping in carbon skeleton. The doped C─S, C═S bonds could introduce suitable defects and active sites, reduces the irreversible capture of Na-ions during storing, and enhanced adsorption performance. While the stable porous carbon skeleton with C═S doped and secondary sulfidation treatment maintained the cycling stability. This study shows that synergistically-doped C─S and C═S bonds in carbon anode can accelerate fast electron transfer and increase the durable storage ability and provide new insights into the bonding doping effects on the carbon materials for durable and fast SIBs system.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202400020-sec-0040-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
